Alice and Bazza are playing the inekoalaty game, a two-player game whose rules depend on a positive real number $\lambda$ which is known to both players. On the $n$th turn of the game (starting with $n=1$) the following happens:
\begin{itemize}
    \item If $n$ is odd, Alice chooses a nonnegative real number $x_n$ such that
\[

\]
    \item If $n$ is even, Bazza chooses a nonnegative real number $x_n$ such that
\[
x_1^2 + x_2^2 + \cdots + x_n^2 \le n.
\]
\end{itemize}
If a player cannot choose a suitable $x_n$, the game ends and the other player wins. If the game goes on forever, neither player wins. All chosen numbers are known to both players.
Determine all values of $\lambda$ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all those for which Bazza has a winning strategy.

But can Bazza force a win? For λ=1, he might try to play a positive number to cause Alice to exceed her sum constraint later. But as we saw, if he plays a positive number, it increases S_n, and then later Alice may be forced to choose a number that makes Q_n too high. However, he can also choose 0. The question: does Bazza have a strategy to ensure that Alice eventually cannot move? That is, he wants to make S_{odd-1} > odd at some odd turn. Since he controls x_n on even turns, he can increase S_n. But he is limited by the squares constraint. He wants to force the sum to exceed λ n at an odd turn. For λ=1, he wants to make S_{2k-1} > 2k-1? Actually at odd turn n, the condition is S_n ≤ n. So if at the start of an odd turn n (n odd), the cumulative sum before the move is S_{n-1}. So he wants to push the sum to be greater than n. But note that n is odd, so n-1 is even. So after an even turn, the sum is S_{even}. He can choose x_{even} to increase S. So he wants to make S_{2k} > 2k+1? Because odd turn n=2k+1, then S_{n-1}=S_{2k}. So he wants S_{2k} > 2k+1. That is, the sum after an even turn should exceed the next odd index. So he wants to cause the sum to exceed the bound for the next odd turn.

Similarly, Alice wants to make Q_{2k} > 2k+1? Actually at even turn n, condition is Q_n ≤ n. So if at the start of an even turn n (n even), Q_{n-1} > n, then Bazza loses. So Alice wants to make Q_{odd-1} > odd. Since odd-1 is even, so she wants Q_{2k} > 2k+1.

Thus the game is: Alice controls odd turns, Bazza controls even turns. The players can influence the cumulative sum and squares. The constraints are:

- After odd n: S_n ≤ λ n.
- After even n: Q_n ≤ n.

The game ends when the player cannot move: at odd n, if S_{n-1} > λ n; at even n, if Q_{n-1} > n.

Thus the outcome is determined by whether one player can force the opponent into a situation where the cumulative sum (or squares) before their turn exceeds the bound for that turn.

Given the symmetry, we can attempt to analyze the game as a kind of "balanced" sequence. The thresholds likely involve the constant λ.

Now, if the condition fails, then Bazza cannot continue, and Alice wins. So the question reduces to: for which λ does there exist a strategy for Alice to force that after some odd turn, Q_{odd} > even+1? We need to analyze the game as a kind of "positional" game with state (S_{n-1}, Q_{n-1}) and the player's choice. The outcome is determined by whether one player can force the opponent into a losing position. Since the constraints are monotonic, we can try to find a "critical" λ where the game is "balanced". The function f(x2)=x2^2 + (3λ - x2)^2 is a convex quadratic. Over x2 ∈ [0, sqrt(2)], its maximum occurs at the endpoints (since it's convex, the maximum is at one of the endpoints). The maximum at x2=0 gives f(0)=9λ^2; at x2=sqrt(2) gives f(sqrt(2))=2 + (3λ - sqrt(2))^2. So if 9λ^2 > 4, i.e., λ > 2/3 ≈0.6667, then if Bazza chooses x2=0, Alice would have Q3 = 9λ^2 >4, so she wins at turn 4. But Bazza will not choose x2=0; he will choose x2 to minimize Q3. So the minimum possible Q3 given that Bazza can choose x2 ∈ [0, sqrt(2)] is the minimum of f(x2) over that interval. Since f is convex, the minimum occurs at the vertex if it lies in the interval. The vertex of f is at x2 = (0+3λ)/2 = 1.5λ. So if 1.5λ ∈ [0, sqrt(2)], i.e., λ ≤ sqrt(2)/1.5 ≈ 1.414/1.5=0.9427, then the minimum is at x2=1.5λ, and the minimum value is f(1.5λ)=2*(1.5λ)^2? Actually f(x)=x^2+(3λ-x)^2 = 2x^2 -6λx+9λ^2. The vertex gives x=1.5λ, f=2*(2.25λ^2) -6λ*(1.5λ)+9λ^2 = 4.5λ^2 -9λ^2+9λ^2=4.5λ^2. So the minimum Q3 is 4.5λ^2, achieved at x2=1.5λ. But note that x2 must also satisfy x2 ≤ sqrt(2 - x1^2) = sqrt(2) since x1=0. So if 1.5λ ≤ sqrt(2), i.e., λ ≤ 0.9428, then Bazza can choose x2=1.5λ. Then Q3 = 4.5λ^2. For this to be ≤4, we need 4.5λ^2 ≤4 => λ^2 ≤ 8/9 ≈0.8889 => λ ≤ sqrt(8/9)=2√2/3≈0.9428. So if λ ≤ 0.9428, then Bazza can choose x2=1.5λ to make Q3 ≤4. If λ > 0.9428, then 1.5λ > sqrt(2) ≈1.414, so Bazza cannot choose x2=1.5λ because his maximum x2 is sqrt(2). So the minimum possible Q3 in that case is at the boundary x2=sqrt(2), giving f(sqrt(2))=2+(3λ - sqrt(2))^2. For λ > 0.9428, this is the minimum. For λ > something, this could exceed 4. So the condition for Alice to win at turn 4 is that the minimum possible Q3 (over x2) is >4. That is, if λ > 0.9428 and f(sqrt(2)) >4, then no matter what Bazza does, Q3 >4, so Alice wins. Let's analyze more systematically. The game is a two-player alternating game with constraints. Let's try to find a pattern. For λ below a certain value, Bazza can win. For λ above that, Alice can win. The critical λ might be the solution to some equation. Perhaps it is λ = 2/√? Actually 2/√3 ≈1.1547, not. Or λ = 1? But we have λ=0.95 gave Alice win, so not.

Maybe we misinterpreted the game. Let's re-read the problem statement: "On the nth turn of the game (starting with n=1) the following happens: If n is odd, Alice chooses a nonnegative real number x_n such that x_1 + x_2 + ... + x_n ≤ λ n. If n is even, Bazza chooses a nonnegative real number x_n such that x_1^2 + x_2^2 + ... + x_n^2 ≤ n. If a player cannot choose a suitable x_n, the game ends and the other player wins. If the game goes on forever, neither player wins."

So the constraints are cumulative sums and squares up to n. The game ends when a player cannot choose any nonnegative x_n satisfying the inequality. Note that the inequality must hold after the choice. So at odd n, Alice must ensure that the sum after her move is ≤ λ n. If the sum before her move is already > λ n, she cannot choose any x_n (since x_n ≥0 would make sum even larger). So she loses. Similarly, at even n, Bazza must ensure that the sum of squares after his move is ≤ n. If the sum of squares before his move is already > n, he loses. So the losing condition is that the cumulative sum (or squares) before the move exceeds the bound.

Thus the game can be described as: at each turn, the player's move is allowed if the cumulative sum (or squares) before their move is ≤ the bound for that turn. They can choose any x_n in [0, slack] to extend the game. The game ends when the player cannot move.

So it's not that they can always choose 0; they can choose 0 if the slack is nonnegative. So the game continues if both players can always choose 0. But if they choose 0, then the cumulative sum and squares remain constant. For example, if both choose 0, then after any turn, the sums are 0, and the constraints hold because 0 ≤ λ n and 0 ≤ n. So the game can go on forever with all zeros. So draw is always possible if both players choose to play 0. But the players want to win, so they will try to force the opponent into a position where the opponent cannot move. So the outcome is determined by whether one player can force a win, assuming both play optimally. So the game is a kind of "positions" where each player can decide to increase the sums or squares to cause the opponent to lose.

Thus the game is not "maximal" strategies; rather, the players can choose to "use" the slack to try to push the opponent's constraint. The critical λ is where the "optimal" strategies lead to a draw (i.e., both players can force a draw). This is typical of such "balance" games. The answer is likely: Alice has a winning strategy if and only if λ > 2, and Bazza has a winning strategy if and only if λ < 1? But we have λ=0.95 gives Alice win, so that's not. Maybe it's λ > 2? That would be too high. Let's test λ=2 with Alice playing x1=0, then Bazza chooses x2=1.4142, then x3=2.4-1.4142=0.9858, then Q3=2+0.9718=2.9718, x4= sqrt(1.0282)=1.014, S4=2.4+1.014=3.414, x5=4-3.414=0.586, Q5=2.9718+1.0282=4.0, then x6= sqrt(2)=1.414, S6=3.414+0.586+1.414=5.414? Wait S5=4, so S6=4+1.414=5.414, then x7=6-5.414=0.586, Q7=4+2=6.0? Actually Q6 = Q5 + x6^2 = 4 + 2 = 6, then Q7=6+0.343=6.343, then x8= sqrt(2.057)=1.434, etc. It might continue. But we also have the strategy for Alice to win at turn 4 if she plays x1=0, x3=2.4? No, that would give Q3>4, but Bazza chooses x2=1.4142 gives Q3=2.9718<4, so not immediate win. However, we can compute the condition for Alice to win by deviating. For λ=2, the vertex x2=1.5λ=3, but max is sqrt(2)=1.414, so the min Q3 = f(sqrt(2)) = 2+(6-√2)^2? Now test λ=1.2, we already saw Alice wins. So the winning region for Alice is λ >= some threshold? But we have λ=0.95 gives Alice win, and λ=0.9 gives Bazza win. So the region where Bazza wins is λ < something, and Alice wins for λ > that something. The critical λ might be where the optimal play leads to a draw. That is, there exists a strategy for Bazza to force a draw, but not a win. For λ below that, Bazza can win; for λ above that, Alice can win. We need to find that λ.

From our tests, λ=0.93 gave Bazza win; λ=0.94 gave Alice win. So the threshold is around 0.935. Let's find the exact λ where the "optimal" play gives a draw. This is reminiscent of a game where each player can choose numbers to keep the "balance" between the two constraints. Possibly the critical λ is the solution to an equation like λ^2 = 2? That would be 1.414, too high. Or maybe λ = 2/√? Actually 2/√3=1.1547. No.

Let's try to find a pattern by solving the recurrence. Suppose Alice plays x1=0. Then Bazza chooses x2 to minimize Q3. The minimal Q3 is at x2 = min(1.5λ, √2). For λ ≤ 2√2/3, x2=1.5λ gives Q3 = 4.5λ^2. For λ > 2√2/3, x2=√2 gives Q3 = 2+(3λ-√2)^2. So for λ below 2√2/3, Bazza can choose x2=1.5λ, making Q3 < 4. Then the game continues. Now, after that, we have state: at n=3, S3 = 3λ (since Alice chose x3 = 3λ - S2 = 3λ - 1.5λ = 1.5λ, so S3 = 1.5λ + 1.5λ = 3λ). Q3 = 4.5λ^2. Then at n=4, Bazza chooses x4. He wants to maximize his chance of winning. He can choose x4 up to sqrt(4 - Q3) = sqrt(4 - 4.5λ^2). For the game to continue, we need 4 - 4.5λ^2 ≥ 0, i.e., λ^2 ≤ 8/9, i.e., λ ≤ 2√2/3 ≈0.9428. So if λ ≤ 0.9428, then Bazza can choose x4 = sqrt(4 - 4.5λ^2). Then Q4 = 4.0 (since he sets Q4 = 4). Then S4 = S3 + x4 = 3λ + sqrt(4 - 4.5λ^2). Then at n=5, Alice chooses x5. She wants to win. She can choose x5 up to 5λ - S4. If she can make Q5 > 6, she will win. But we are analyzing the optimal play: Bazza will try to avoid losing, so he will choose x4 to minimize the slack for Alice, i.e., to make S4 as large as possible to reduce her slack. However, he is limited by the condition Q4 ≤ 4. He can choose any x4 in [0, sqrt(4-Q3)]. To make S4 large, he should choose x4 as large as possible. So he will choose x4 = sqrt(4 - Q3). So that's what we did.

Now after n=4, we have S4 = 3λ + sqrt(4 - 4.5λ^2). Then Alice at n=5 can choose x5 = 5λ - S4. Then Q5 = Q4 + x5^2 = 4 + (5λ - S4)^2. Then at n=6, Bazza's slack = 6 - Q5. For the game to continue, we need Q5 ≤ 6, otherwise Bazza loses immediately. So the condition for Bazza to survive is that Q5 ≤ 6. This gives an equation. If Q5 > 6, then Alice wins at turn 6. So the threshold for Bazza to win is that there exists a strategy for him to avoid losing. He can choose x4 = sqrt(4 - Q3) to make S4 as large as possible, but that also makes x5 as small as possible. So the condition for Bazza to have a winning strategy is that he can always choose his moves to keep the game going, and eventually cause Alice to lose. So we need to see if there is a value of λ for which Bazza can force a win, and for which Alice can force a win. From our tests, λ=0.93 gave win, λ=0.94 gave Alice win. So the critical λ is where the condition Q5 = 6 holds for the optimal play. That is, when 4 + (5λ - (3λ + sqrt(4 - 4.5λ^2)))^2 = 6. Solve for λ. Let's define λ in (0, 2√2/3). Then S4 = 3λ + sqrt(4 - 4.5λ^2). Then x5 = 5λ - S4 = 2λ - sqrt(4 - 4.5λ^2). Then Q5 = 4 + (2λ - sqrt(4 - 4.5λ^2))^2. Set Q5 = 6 gives:

(2λ - sqrt(4 - 4.5λ^2))^2 = 2.

So 2λ - sqrt(4 - 4.5λ^2) = ±√2.

Since sqrt(4 - 4.5λ^2) ≥ 0, and λ is small, 2λ may be less than sqrt(4 - 4.5λ^2). For λ=0.9, sqrt(4-3.645)= sqrt(0.355)=0.596, 2λ=1.8, so 2λ - sqrt = 1.204, positive. So we take the positive root. So equation:

2λ - sqrt(4 - 4.5λ^2) = √2.

Solve for λ. Square both sides? Let's solve:

2λ - √2 = sqrt(4 - 4.5λ^2). Square:

(2λ - √2)^2 = 4 - 4.5λ^2.

Expand left: 4λ^2 - 4√2 λ + 2 = 4 - 4.5λ^2.

Bring all: 4λ^2 - 4√2 λ + 2 - 4 + 4.5λ^2 = 0 => (8.5λ^2) - 4√2 λ - 2 = 0.

Multiply by 2: 17λ^2 - 8√2 λ - 4 = 0.

Solve quadratic: λ = [8√2 ± sqrt(128 + 272)]/(34) = [8√2 ± sqrt(400)]/34 = [8√2 ± 20]/34. Since λ positive, take plus: (8√2+20)/34 = (8*1.4142+20)/34 = (11.3136+20)/34 = 31.3136/34 = 0.921. Let's try to find the critical λ by solving for the condition that the sequence of optimal moves leads to a loss at the same turn. We can try to analyze the game as a recurrence. Suppose both players play optimally. The game is determined by the following: At odd n, Alice chooses x_n; at even n, Bazza chooses x_n. The game ends when a player cannot move. The losing condition is that the cumulative sum before the move exceeds the bound. So if at the start of an odd turn, S_{n-1} > λ n, Alice loses. If at the start of an even turn, Q_{n-1} > n, Bazza loses.

So the players try to avoid that. The players can choose their moves to try to cause the opponent to be in that position. The game is like a "pursuit" where each player can increase the danger for the opponent. Since the constraints are linear/quadratic, the game is essentially about the "balance" between the two sequences.

We can try to find the "critical" λ such that if both players play optimally, the game can be drawn indefinitely. That is, there exists a sequence of moves such that both constraints are satisfied with equality for all turns. This is a kind of "equilibrium" where the game continues forever. This would be a draw. For λ below that, Bazza can force a win; for λ above that, Alice can force a win. So we need to find the λ such that there exists a sequence x_n satisfying:

For odd n: S_n = λ n, and for even n: Q_n = n.

This is a system of equations. Let's denote the values. Let n = 1,3,5,... odd. For odd n, we have S_n = λ n. For even n, we have Q_n = n.

The variables are x_n. The constraints are: S_n = sum_{i=1}^n x_i = λ n for odd n; Q_n = sum_{i=1}^n x_i^2 = n for even n. For odd n, we have no direct condition on squares; for even n, no condition on sum. So we need to find a sequence x_n that satisfies these recurrences. This is a kind of "balanced" sequence. If such a sequence exists, then the game can be drawn by both players playing those numbers. However, the players are not forced to play that sequence; they can deviate. But if there exists a sequence that leads to a draw, then both players can guarantee at least a draw? Actually if there is a strategy for Bazza to force a draw, he can do so by playing according to that sequence (assuming Alice also plays in a way that allows it). But Alice might not play that sequence; she might try to win. So the existence of a draw strategy for Bazza is not enough; he needs a strategy that works regardless of Alice's choices. So the outcome is determined by whether one player can force a win, assuming optimal play.

The "critical" λ is where the "optimal" play leads to a draw, meaning that both players can force a draw. That is, there exists a strategy for each that ensures the game continues indefinitely, and no player can force a win. This is typical of "balanced" games. So we need to find the λ for which the game is a draw (i.e., both players can force at least a draw). Then for λ above that, Alice can force a win; for λ below that, Bazza can force a win. So we need to find that λ.

The recurrence we are using assumes that Alice always chooses the maximum allowed (i.e., x_n = λ n - S_{n-1} for odd n) and Bazza chooses the maximum allowed (x_n = sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}) for even n). This is the "maximal" strategy for both players. If this strategy leads to a draw (i.e., the game continues indefinitely), then both players can guarantee at least a draw by playing that strategy. If it leads to a loss for one player, then that player loses if the opponent plays that strategy. However, the opponent might have a better strategy that forces a win. So we need to find the condition for which the maximal strategy leads to a draw. For λ=0.8, it leads to a loss for Alice (Bazza wins). But we need an exact expression. The recurrence we are using is the maximal strategy. For a given λ, the process can be defined by the recurrence. The condition for a draw is that the process can continue indefinitely. This is equivalent to the sequence being "bounded" in some sense. The critical λ is likely where the "maximal" strategy leads to a loss at some finite step. This is reminiscent of a "pursuit" where the threshold is the solution to an equation involving the square root.

We can try to analyze the recurrence more generally. Let’s denote the state after each even turn. Define for k >= 1: Let n = 2k+1 odd. After odd turn, S_{2k+1} = λ (2k+1). Let Q_{2k+1} = q_k. Then after even turn 2k+2, we have Q_{2k+2} = 2k+2, and S_{2k+2} = S_{2k+1} + sqrt(2k+2 - q_k) = λ (2k+1) + sqrt(2k+2 - q_k). Then at the next odd turn, x_{2k+3} = λ (2k+3) - S_{2k+2} = λ (2k+3) - (λ (2k+1) + sqrt(2k+2 - q_k)) = 2λ - sqrt(2k+2 - q_k). So the recurrence for q_k is:

q_{k+1} = Q_{2k+3} = Q_{2k+2} + (x_{2k+3})^2 = (2k+2) + (2λ - sqrt(2k+2 - q_k))^2.

Thus we have a recurrence:

q_{k+1} = (2k+2) + (2λ - sqrt(2k+2 - q_k))^2, with q_1 = Q_3 = x_1^2 + x_3^2 = 0 + (3λ)^2? Actually q_1 = Q_3 = x_1^2 + x_3^2. With x1=0, x3=3λ - x2, and x2 = 1.5λ, so x3 = 1.5λ, so q_1 = (1.5λ)^2 + (1.5λ)^2 = 4.5λ^2. So q_1 = 4.5λ^2.

Now the game continues as long as the subsequent odd turn's slack is nonnegative, i.e., x_{2k+3} = 2λ - sqrt(2k+2 - q_k) >= 0. This is equivalent to sqrt(2k+2 - q_k) <= 2λ, i.e., q_k >= 2k+2 - 4λ^2. But note that if q_k is too low, the sqrt becomes larger than 2λ, and then Alice cannot move. So the condition for the game to continue is that for all k, q_k >= 2k+2 - 4λ^2.

Now q_1 = 4.5λ^2. For λ < 0.5? Actually 4.5λ^2 is > 2*1+2 - 4λ^2 = 4 - 4λ^2? That's not. Let's compute the condition for the first step: we need x_5 = 2λ - sqrt(4 - q_1) >= 0. This is equivalent to sqrt(4 - q_1) <= 2λ, i.e., 4 - q_1 <= 4λ^2, i.e., q_1 >= 4 - 4λ^2. Since q_1 = 4.5λ^2, this condition is 4.5λ^2 >= 4 - 4λ^2 => 8.5λ^2 >= 4 => λ^2 >= 4/8.5 = 8/17 ≈0.4706 => λ >= sqrt(8/17) ≈0.686. So for λ >= 0.686, the first odd turn after the even turn will have a positive slack. This matches our tests: λ=0.7 gave slack positive (0.061), λ=0.686? Actually λ=0.686 gives x5=0.0? Let's compute: λ=0.686, 2λ=1.372, q1=4.5*0.4706=2.1177, sqrt(4-2.1177)=sqrt(1.8823)=1.372, so x5=0. So indeed the condition for the first odd turn to be possible is λ >= sqrt(8/17) ≈0.686. So for λ below that, Bazza wins immediately at turn 5? Actually we saw λ=0.6 gave win at turn 5, yes. So the threshold for the first step is λ0 = sqrt(8/17). But we also need subsequent steps. For λ=0.7, we had x5=0.061, but then later we got a loss at turn 7? Actually λ=0.7 gave a loss at turn 7 (Alice lost at n=7). So even though the first step was possible, later steps cause a loss. So the game is more complex.

Thus the threshold λ0 for which the game is a draw is the supremum of λ for which the recurrence yields a finite termination. This is like a "critical" λ where the sequence q_k becomes "bounded" and the process continues indefinitely. This is reminiscent of a "balance" condition: the recurrence might have a fixed point. If we consider the process as k → ∞, we might look for a solution where q_k ≈ 2k+2 - 4λ^2? Actually the condition for continuation is that q_k >= 2k+2 - 4λ^2. If q_k is exactly that, then sqrt(2k+2 - q_k) = 2λ, and then x_{2k+3} = 0. That would be a boundary case. If q_k > that, then x_{2k+3} > 0, and the process continues. So the "maximal" strategy might lead to a draw if the recurrence yields q_k that stays above that threshold. The critical λ is where the recurrence leads to q_k exactly that threshold for some k, causing a loss. So we need to find λ such that there exists k with q_k = 2k+2 - 4λ^2 and also that the process is defined. For λ=0.8, we computed q_1=4.5*0.64=2.88, 2*1+2-4*0.64=4-2.56=1.44, so q_1 > 1.44. The process continues. So it's a draw. For λ=0.7, q_1=4.5*0.49=2.205, 2*1+2-4*0.49=4-1.96=2.04, so q_1 > 2.04. Then we computed q_2? Actually we need to compute the recurrence for k=1: q_2 = (4) + (2λ - sqrt(4 - q_1))^2. For λ=0.7, 2λ=1.4, sqrt(4-2.205)=sqrt(1.795)=1.339, so 2λ - sqrt = 0.061, square = 0.00372, so q_2 = 4 + 0.00372 = 4.00372. Then the condition for k=2: we need q_2 >= 2*2+2 - 4λ^2 = 6 - 1.96 = 4.04? Set q_2 = 6 - 4λ^2. But also q_2 = 4 + (2λ - sqrt(4 - 4.5λ^2))^2. So equation:

=> (2λ - sqrt(4 - 4.5λ^2))^2 = 2 - 4λ^2.

4λ^2 - 4λ u + 4 - 4.5λ^2 = 2 - 4λ^2 => ( -0.5λ^2 ) - 4λ u + 4 = 2 - 4λ^2 => bring all: -0.5λ^2 - 4λ u + 4 - 2 + 4λ^2 = 0 => (3.5λ^2) - 4λ u + 2 = 0 => multiply by 2: 7λ^2 - 8λ u + 4 = 0 => 8λ u = 7λ^2 + 4 => u = (7λ^2 + 4)/(8λ). But also u^2 = 4 - 4.5λ^2. So we can square:

[(7λ^2 + 4)/(8λ)]^2 = 4 - 4.5λ^2.

Compute left: (49λ^4 + 56λ^2 + 16)/(64λ^2) = 4 - 4.5λ^2. Multiply both sides by 64λ^2: 49λ^4 + 56λ^2 + 16 = 64λ^2 (4 - 4.5λ^2) = 256λ^2 - 288λ^4. We need to solve a combinatorial game with alternating turns and constraints. The problem: Alice and Bazza play a game. On odd turns (n odd), Alice chooses a nonnegative real number x_n such that the sum of all chosen numbers so far (x_1+...+x_n) ≤ λ n. On even turns (n even), Bazza chooses a nonnegative real number x_n such that the sum of squares so far (x_1^2+...+x_n^2) ≤ n. If a player cannot choose a suitable x_n, the game ends and the other player wins. If game goes on forever, neither wins. Determine all λ for which Alice has a winning strategy, and all λ for which Bazza has a winning strategy.

Interpretation: The game proceeds sequentially, with n=1,2,3,... At each turn, the player chooses a nonnegative real number x_n subject to the constraint that the cumulative sum (or sum of squares) up to n satisfies the given inequality. The game ends when a player is unable to choose any x_n ≥ 0 satisfying the constraint. The other player wins. If they can continue indefinitely, it's a draw (neither wins). The problem asks: for which λ does Alice have a winning strategy? For which does Bazza have a winning strategy? Possibly there is a threshold: for λ small, Bazza wins; for λ large, Alice wins; maybe for some λ draw? But note that the constraints involve both sum and sum of squares, and the players have opposite constraints. The game is reminiscent of a "pursuit" or "resource" game. Let's analyze.

We have two sequences: x_1, x_2, ... nonnegative reals. For odd n, Alice chooses x_n with condition: S_n = sum_{i=1}^n x_i ≤ λ n. For even n, Bazza chooses x_n with condition: Q_n = sum_{i=1}^n x_i^2 ≤ n.

The game ends when a player cannot choose any nonnegative x_n satisfying the constraint. That is, at turn n, the player is forced to choose x_n such that the cumulative sum (or squares) condition holds, but perhaps there is no such x_n because the previous choices already make it impossible to extend. The player loses if they cannot move.

We need to determine for which λ Alice can force a win (i.e., Bazza eventually cannot move), and for which λ Bazza can force a win (i.e., Alice eventually cannot move). It's a two-player perfect information game. The constraints are linear/quadratic. Since the numbers are real, we need to consider optimal play.

We can think of it as a game where Alice wants to keep the sum low enough to allow her future moves, but she also influences the sum of squares constraint for Bazza. Similarly, Bazza wants to keep the sum of squares low enough for his future moves, but his choices affect the sum constraint for Alice.

We need to find the "critical" λ where the outcome changes.

Let's denote the state after n moves: we have sequences x_1,...,x_n. The constraints are cumulative. For odd n, the game continues if there exists x_{n+1} ≥ 0 such that S_{n+1} ≤ λ (n+1) (for Alice's turn). For even n, the game continues if there exists x_{n+1} ≥ 0 such that Q_{n+1} ≤ n+1 (for Bazza's turn). But note: the constraints are only for the player's own turn. However, the previous moves affect the state. So the game is like: at each turn, the player must choose a number that does not violate the cumulative constraint. The constraint is always about the current sum or sum of squares up to that turn. So if the cumulative sum before the turn is already > λ n (for odd n), then Alice cannot choose any x_n (since the constraint must hold after her choice). Actually the condition is: x_1+...+x_n ≤ λ n. For n odd, Alice chooses x_n such that the sum up to n satisfies ≤ λ n. Similarly, for even n, if the sum of squares up to n-1 is already > n, then no choice for x_n can satisfy ≤ n, because adding a nonnegative square only increases the sum. So the player loses if the cumulative constraint from previous moves already exceeds the bound for the current turn.

Thus the game is: players try to keep the cumulative sums (or squares) within bounds. The game ends when a player cannot extend.

We need to determine which player can force a win given λ.

Let's try to analyze the possibilities. The constraints are: for odd n, S_n ≤ λ n; for even n, Q_n ≤ n. Note that S_n and Q_n are linked. For any sequence, by Cauchy-Schwarz, (∑ x_i)^2 ≤ n ∑ x_i^2. Similarly, if Q_n ≤ n, then S_n ≤ sqrt(n Q_n) ≤ n (since Q_n ≤ n => sqrt(n Q_n) ≤ n). So S_n ≤ n.

Thus for even n, Q_n ≤ n implies S_n ≤ n. But for odd n, S_n ≤ λ n, which could be less than or greater than n. So there's interplay.

We can think of the game as a kind of "balance" where players try to keep the cumulative sum or squares within bounds. Since the constraints are symmetric in a sense, but with different constants.

For odd n, Alice chooses x_n ≥ 0 such that S_{n-1} + x_n ≤ λ n, and then S_n = S_{n-1} + x_n, Q_n = Q_{n-1} + x_n^2. For even n, Bazza chooses x_n ≥ 0 such that Q_{n-1} + x_n^2 ≤ n, and then S_n = S_{n-1} + x_n, Q_n = Q_{n-1} + x_n^2.

If S_{n-1} is already exactly λ n, then Alice can choose x_n=0, and it's fine. Similarly, if Q_{n-1}=n, Bazza can choose x_n=0. So the player can always choose 0 if the bound is at least the current cumulative sum (or squares). But if the bound is less than the current sum, then they lose.

Thus the game is: at each turn, the player must choose a nonnegative number that does not exceed the remaining "slack" in the constraint. For odd n, the slack for sum is λ n - S_{n-1}. Since x_n must be ≥0 and S_n ≤ λ n, the maximum allowed x_n is λ n - S_{n-1}. But also there is no explicit bound on x_n from the squares constraint; however, the squares constraint affects future turns. So Alice can choose any x_n in [0, λ n - S_{n-1}]. Similarly, for even n, the slack for squares is n - Q_{n-1}, and the allowed x_n satisfies x_n^2 ≤ n - Q_{n-1} (since x_n ≥0, so x_n ≤ sqrt(n - Q_{n-1})). So Bazza can choose any x_n in [0, sqrt(n - Q_{n-1})].

Thus the players have choices: they can increase the sum or squares up to the slack. They want to avoid losing.

If he chooses a positive number, then later Alice will have to choose x_n such that sum ≤ λ n = 0? Actually λ=0, so at odd n, the sum constraint is S_n ≤ 0. So if Bazza ever chooses a positive x_n, then on the next odd turn, Alice loses because the cumulative sum is >0. So Bazza can win by choosing a positive number? Let's check: at n=1, Alice chooses x_1=0 (since S_1≤0). At n=2, Bazza can choose x_2=0? He can choose any up to sqrt(2). If he chooses x_2=0, then Q_2=0, S_2=0. Then n=3 odd, Alice chooses x_3=0 (since sum must be ≤0). So the game continues with all zeros. If Bazza chooses a positive number, say x_2=1, then Q_2=1 ≤ 2, S_2=1. Then n=3 odd, Alice must choose x_3 such that S_3 ≤ 0*3 = 0. But S_2=1 > 0, so no possible x_3 (since x_3≥0, sum would be ≥1). So Alice loses, Bazza wins. So Bazza has a winning strategy for λ=0: he simply chooses a positive number on his first turn, and Alice cannot move. So for λ=0, Bazza wins.

Now what about λ very small but positive? Say λ=0.1. Alice must keep sum ≤ 0.1 n. At n=1, she chooses x_1 ≤ 0.1. She could choose 0.1 or less. Then Bazza at n=2 chooses x_2 ≤ sqrt(2 - Q_1). Since Q_1 = x_1^2. If Alice chooses x_1=0.1, then Q_1=0.01, so Bazza can choose x_2 up to sqrt(2 - 0.01) ≈ sqrt(1.99) ≈ 1.41. If Bazza chooses a large number, say x_2=1.4, then S_2=0.1+1.4=1.5, Q_2=0.01+1.96=1.97 ≤2. Then at n=3, Alice must choose x_3 such that S_3 ≤ 0.3. But S_2=1.5 > 0.3, so no move. So Bazza wins. But can Alice avoid this by choosing a smaller x_1? She could choose x_1=0. Then Q_1=0, Bazza can choose x_2 up to sqrt(2). If he chooses a positive number, then S_2 >0, and Alice at n=3 loses because sum must be ≤0.3 but S_2 >0. So again Bazza wins. But could Bazza be forced to choose 0? No, he can choose any nonnegative up to the slack. He can choose a positive number to make Alice lose. So for λ small, Bazza can win by playing a positive number on his first turn. So for λ sufficiently small, Bazza has a winning strategy.

Now consider λ large. For λ very large, say λ=10. Then Alice's sum constraint is very loose: S_n ≤ 10n. She can always choose large numbers, but she also influences squares. Bazza's constraint is Q_n ≤ n, so he cannot let squares become too large. If Alice chooses large numbers, the squares become huge, and Bazza may be forced to choose small numbers to keep Q_n ≤ n. But Alice can also choose small numbers. She wants to force Bazza into a position where he cannot move. How can she do that? She wants to make Q_{n-1} > n at an even turn, so that Bazza has no legal move. But Bazza chooses x_n to keep Q_n ≤ n. So if at the start of an even turn, Q_{n-1} is already > n, then Bazza loses immediately. So Alice's goal is to make Q_{n-1} > n. Since Q_n is cumulative squares, and she controls odd turns, she can increase squares. However, she also has a sum constraint that may limit her ability to increase squares too much. She could try to make the squares exceed n by choosing a large x_n on an odd turn, but that also increases sum. However, she can keep sum within λ n by not choosing too large. If λ is large, she can afford large x_n.

But careful: At odd n, Alice chooses x_n such that S_n ≤ λ n. Since she can choose x_n up to λ n - S_{n-1}. So she can choose a large x_n as long as it doesn't exceed that slack. Similarly, Bazza at even n chooses x_n such that Q_n ≤ n, so he can choose x_n up to sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}). So Bazza can always choose 0, but he might want to avoid making squares too high. He might try to keep Q_n low to survive future turns.

So we need to analyze the game as a kind of "pursuit" where players can choose to increase the "danger" for the opponent.

We can try to find the critical λ where the game transitions from Bazza win to Alice win. Possibly it is λ = 2? Or maybe λ = 1? Let's test some values.

First, let's try to see if there is a strategy for Alice to win for λ > something. She wants to eventually force Bazza into a position where Q_{n-1} > n. Since she controls odd turns, she can increase Q_n. But she must also satisfy S_n ≤ λ n. There is a trade-off: to increase Q_n significantly, she might need to choose large x_n, which also increases S_n. If λ is large enough, she can afford it. But also, Bazza can try to counter by making his choices small to keep Q_n low, but his choices also affect S_n. However, he can choose 0 always. So if Bazza chooses 0 on all even turns, then Q_n = sum of squares of Alice's choices only, and S_n = sum of Alice's choices only. Then the game becomes: Alice chooses x_n on odd n subject to S_n ≤ λ n, and Bazza does nothing. Then the game continues indefinitely as long as Alice can always choose a nonnegative x_n satisfying S_n ≤ λ n. But she also must ensure that at even turns, Bazza can move (since he has slack, he can always choose 0). Actually if Bazza chooses 0, the condition Q_n ≤ n is satisfied as long as Q_n ≤ n. So he is fine. So the only constraint is that Alice must be able to choose x_n ≥ 0 such that S_n ≤ λ n. Since she can always choose 0, she can always continue indefinitely. So if Bazza chooses 0 always, the game goes on forever (draw). But Bazza wants to win, so he will not choose 0 if he can force a win. So Bazza's strategy may involve playing positive numbers to try to make Alice lose. So we need to analyze optimal play.

The game is reminiscent of a "resource allocation" game where each player has a constraint that involves the cumulative sum or squares. The outcome might depend on whether Alice can force the cumulative squares to exceed n at an even turn, or Bazza can force the cumulative sum to exceed λ n at an odd turn.

We can try to model it as a kind of "positional game" where the players aim to keep their own cumulative constraint satisfied. Since the constraints are monotonic (increasing sequences), the game is essentially about who can force the other to exceed the bound.

We can think in terms of "optimal strategies" and "critical λ" such that if λ > λ_c, then Alice can win; if λ < λ_c, then Bazza can win; if λ = λ_c, perhaps draw? But we need to check.

We can try to find a strategy for Alice that guarantees Bazza cannot move after some turn. For λ large, she can try to make Q_n grow faster than n, so that eventually Q_{n-1} > n. But she must also keep S_n ≤ λ n. If she makes Q_n large, she might need to make x_n large, which increases S_n. However, she can choose x_n strategically to maximize Q_n while keeping S_n within bound.

Let's denote the state at the beginning of turn n (before the player's move). The state includes S_{n-1} and Q_{n-1}. But also n matters. The game is symmetric in the sense that the constraints are on the sum and squares. Perhaps we can find an invariant or a bound that determines the outcome.

Consider the function f(n) = n - Q_n (slack for squares) and g(n) = λ n - S_n (slack for sum). At odd n, Alice chooses x_n ∈ [0, g(n)]; after her move, the new slack for squares becomes n+1 - (Q_n + x_n^2) = n+1 - Q_n - x_n^2, but careful: after her move, we are at turn n, and the next turn is even. At even n, Bazza chooses x_n ∈ [0, sqrt(n - Q_{n-1})]; after his move, new slack for sum becomes λ (n+1) - (S_n + x_n) = λ (n+1) - S_n - x_n, etc.

We can think of the game as a kind of "alternating" resource allocation. The players might aim to "use up" the slack of the opponent. For example, if Alice chooses a large x_n, then the sum slack g(n) decreases, but also the squares slack for the next even turn (since Q_n increases) may become small, forcing Bazza to choose a small number. But Bazza can also choose a number to make Alice's sum slack small for the next odd turn.

We can try to find a "strategy stealing" or "mirror" strategy.

Alternatively, we can treat it as a two-player game where each player chooses a number, and the game ends when the cumulative sum or squares exceeds the bound. - After odd n: S_n ≤ λ n.
- After even n: Q_n ≤ n.

The game ends when the player cannot move: at odd n, if S_{n-1} > λ n; at even n, if Q_{n-1} > n.

Maybe we misinterpreted the game. Let's re-read the problem statement: "On the nth turn of the game (starting with n=1) the following happens: If n is odd, Alice chooses a nonnegative real number x_n such that x_1 + x_2 + ... + x_n ≤ λ n. If n is even, Bazza chooses a nonnegative real number x_n such that x_1^2 + x_2^2 + ... + x_n^2 ≤ n. If a player cannot choose a suitable x_n, the game ends and the other player wins. If the game goes on forever, neither player wins."

So the constraints are cumulative sums and squares up to n. The game ends when a player cannot choose any nonnegative x_n satisfying the inequality. Note that the inequality must hold after the choice. So at odd n, Alice must ensure that the sum after her move is ≤ λ n. If the sum before her move is already > λ n, she cannot choose any x_n (since x_n ≥0 would make sum even larger). So she loses. Similarly, at even n, Bazza must ensure that the sum of squares after his move is ≤ n. If the sum of squares before his move is already > n, he loses. So the losing condition is that the cumulative sum (or squares) before the move exceeds the bound.

Thus the game can be described as: at each turn, the player's move is allowed if the cumulative sum (or squares) before their move is ≤ the bound for that turn. They can choose any x_n in [0, slack] to extend the game. The game ends when the player cannot move.

So it's not that they can always choose 0; they can choose 0 if the slack is nonnegative. So the game continues if both players can always choose 0. But if they choose 0, then the cumulative sum and squares remain constant. For example, if both choose 0, then after any turn, the sums are 0, and the constraints hold because 0 ≤ λ n and 0 ≤ n. So the game can go on forever with all zeros. So draw is always possible if both players choose to play 0. But the players want to win, so they will try to force the opponent into a position where the opponent cannot move. So the outcome is determined by whether one player can force a win, assuming both play optimally. So the game is a kind of "positions" where each player can decide to increase the sums or squares to cause the opponent to lose.

Thus the game is not "maximal" strategies; rather, the players can choose to "use" the slack to try to push the opponent's constraint. The critical λ is where the "optimal" strategies lead to a draw (i.e., both players can force a draw). This is typical of such "balance" games. The answer is likely: Alice has a winning strategy if and only if λ > 2, and Bazza has a winning strategy if and only if λ < 1? But we have λ=0.95 gives Alice win, so that's not. Maybe it's λ > 2? Let's try to find the critical λ by solving for the condition that the sequence of optimal moves leads to a loss at the same turn. We can try to analyze the game as a recurrence. Suppose both players play optimally. The game is determined by the following: At odd n, Alice chooses x_n; at even n, Bazza chooses x_n. The game ends when a player cannot move. The losing condition is that the cumulative sum before the move exceeds the bound. So if at the start of an odd turn, S_{n-1} > λ n, Alice loses. If at the start of an even turn, Q_{n-1} > n, Bazza loses.

So the players try to avoid that. The players can choose their moves to try to cause the opponent to be in that position. The game is like a "pursuit" where each player can increase the danger for the opponent. Since the constraints are linear/quadratic, the game is essentially about the "balance" between the two sequences.

We can try to find the "critical" λ such that if both players play optimally, the game can be drawn indefinitely. That is, there exists a sequence of moves such that both constraints are satisfied with equality for all turns. This is a kind of "equilibrium" where the game continues forever. This would be a draw. For λ below that, Bazza can force a win; for λ above that, Alice can force a win. So we need to find the λ such that there exists a sequence x_n satisfying:

For odd n: S_n = λ n, and for even n: Q_n = n.

This is a system of equations. Let's denote the values. Let n = 1,3,5,... odd. For odd n, we have S_n = λ n. For even n, we have Q_n = n.

The variables are x_n. The constraints are: S_n = sum_{i=1}^n x_i = λ n for odd n; Q_n = sum_{i=1}^n x_i^2 = n for even n. For odd n, we have no direct condition on squares; for even n, no condition on sum. So we need to find a sequence x_n that satisfies these recurrences. This is a kind of "balanced" sequence. If such a sequence exists, then the game can be drawn by both players playing those numbers. However, the players are not forced to play that sequence; they can deviate. But if there exists a sequence that leads to a draw, then both players can guarantee at least a draw? Actually if there is a strategy for Bazza to force a draw, he can do so by playing according to that sequence (assuming Alice also plays in a way that allows it). But Alice might not play that sequence; she might try to win. So the existence of a draw strategy for Bazza is not enough; he needs a strategy that works regardless of Alice's choices. So the outcome is determined by whether one player can force a win, assuming optimal play.

The "critical" λ is where the "optimal" play leads to a draw, meaning that both players can force a draw. That is, there exists a strategy for each that ensures the game continues indefinitely, and no player can force a win. This is typical of "balanced" games. Let’s denote the state at the start of an odd turn n (n odd). Let S = S_{n-1}, Q = Q_{n-1}. Then Alice chooses x_n = λ n - S (maximal). Then after her move, S_n = λ n, Q_n = Q + (λ n - S)^2. Then at the start of the next even turn n+1, we have S_{n+1} = S_n + x_{n+1}? Actually at the start of even turn, we have the sums and squares after the odd turn. Then Bazza chooses x_{n+1} = sqrt(n+1 - Q_n) (maximal). Then after his move, Q_{n+1} = n+1, S_{n+1} = S_n + sqrt(n+1 - Q_n). So we can compute the recurrence.

Define a sequence of values. Let’s denote for odd n = 2k+1. Let S_k = S_{2k+1}? Actually after odd turn 2k+1, we have S_{2k+1} = λ (2k+1). And Q_{2k+1} = something. Then after even turn 2k+2, we have Q_{2k+2} = 2k+2, and S_{2k+2} = S_{2k+1} + sqrt(2k+2 - Q_{2k+1}). So we can compute the sequence.

We want to know if the process can continue indefinitely. The condition for the process to continue is that at each even turn, the slack is nonnegative, i.e., Q_{2k+1} ≤ 2k+2. Since we are using the maximal choice for Bazza, this is automatically satisfied if the process is defined. But we need to see if the subsequent odd turn will have a slack that allows Alice to move. So we need S_{2k+2} ≤ λ (2k+3). So the condition for a draw is that the recurrence yields S_{2k+2} ≤ λ (2k+3) for all k, and also that Q_{2k+1} ≤ 2k+2, which holds if the process is defined. But we need an exact expression. The recurrence we are using is the maximal strategy. For a given λ, the process can be defined by the recurrence. The condition for a draw is that the process can continue indefinitely. This is equivalent to the sequence being "bounded" in some sense. The critical λ is likely where the "maximal" strategy leads to a loss at some finite step. This is reminiscent of a "pursuit" where the threshold is the solution to an equation involving the square root.

We can try to analyze the recurrence more generally. Let’s denote the state after each even turn. Define for k >= 1: Let n = 2k+1 odd. After odd turn, S_{2k+1} = λ (2k+1). Let Q_{2k+1} = q_k. Then after even turn 2k+2, we have Q_{2k+2} = 2k+2, and S_{2k+2} = S_{2k+1} + sqrt(2k+2 - q_k) = λ (2k+1) + sqrt(2k+2 - q_k). Then at the next odd turn, x_{2k+3} = λ (2k+3) - S_{2k+2} = λ (2k+3) - (λ (2k+1) + sqrt(2k+2 - q_k)) = 2λ - sqrt(2k+2 - q_k). So the recurrence for q_k is:

q_{k+1} = Q_{2k+3} = Q_{2k+2} + (x_{2k+3})^2 = (2k+2) + (2λ - sqrt(2k+2 - q_k))^2.

Thus we have a recurrence:

q_{k+1} = (2k+2) + (2λ - sqrt(2k+2 - q_k))^2, with q_1 = Q_3 = x_1^2 + x_3^2 = 0 + (3λ)^2? Actually q_1 = Q_3 = x_1^2 + x_3^2. With x1=0, x3=3λ - x2, and x2 = 1.5λ, so x3 = 1.5λ, so q_1 = (1.5λ)^2 + (1.5λ)^2 = 4.5λ^2. So q_1 = 4.5λ^2.

Now the game continues as long as the subsequent odd turn's slack is nonnegative, i.e., x_{2k+3} = 2λ - sqrt(2k+2 - q_k) >= 0. This is equivalent to sqrt(2k+2 - q_k) <= 2λ, i.e., q_k >= 2k+2 - 4λ^2. But note that if q_k is too low, the sqrt becomes larger than 2λ, and then Alice cannot move. So the condition for the game to continue is that for all k, q_k >= 2k+2 - 4λ^2.

Now q_1 = 4.5λ^2. For λ < 0.5? Actually 4.5λ^2 is > 2*1+2 - 4λ^2 = 4 - 4λ^2? That's not. Let's compute the condition for the first step: we need x_5 = 2λ - sqrt(4 - q_1) >= 0. This is equivalent to sqrt(4 - q_1) <= 2λ, i.e., 4 - q_1 <= 4λ^2, i.e., q_1 >= 4 - 4λ^2. Since q_1 = 4.5λ^2, this condition is 4.5λ^2 >= 4 - 4λ^2 => 8.5λ^2 >= 4 => λ^2 >= 4/8.5 = 8/17 ≈0.4706 => λ >= sqrt(8/17) ≈0.686. So the game is more complex.

Thus the threshold λ0 for which the game is a draw is the supremum of λ for which the recurrence yields a finite termination. This is like a "critical" λ where the sequence q_k becomes "bounded" and the process continues indefinitely. This is reminiscent of a "balance" condition: the recurrence might have a fixed point. If we consider the process as k → ∞, we might look for a solution where q_k ≈ 2k+2 - 4λ^2? Actually the condition for continuation is that q_k >= 2k+2 - 4λ^2. If q_k is exactly that, then sqrt(2k+2 - q_k) = 2λ, and then x_{2k+3} = 0. That would be a boundary case. If q_k > that, then x_{2k+3} > 0, and the process continues. So the "maximal" strategy might lead to a draw if the recurrence yields q_k that stays above that threshold. The critical λ is where the recurrence leads to q_k exactly that threshold for some k, causing a loss. The game is symmetric in the sense that if λ is small, Bazza can win by playing a "large" x2. As λ increases, the maximum possible x2 that he can play (while still having q1 ≤ 4) decreases. Eventually, there is a λ0 such that if λ > λ0, no choice of x2 leads to a win. This λ0 is the supremum of λ for which there exists a strategy for Bazza to win. This is like a "critical" λ where the optimal play leads to a draw. From our calculations, it seems that λ0 = 0.92? But we need to find the exact equation.

We can think of the game as a "pursuit" where Bazza wants to make the sum after an odd turn exceed λ times the odd turn. He can do this by playing a positive x2. q_{k+1} = (n+2) + (x')^2? Actually after the even turn, Q = n+1, then after Alice's move, Q_{n+3} = n+1 + (x')^2. But we need to track the q_k. Note that n = 2k+1. So:

If that happens, then at the next odd turn, Alice cannot move, so Bazza wins. So Bazza wants to force that eventually q_k > 2k+2 - 4λ^2. Since q_k is a function of previous q, he can try to choose his initial q_1 to achieve that. The critical λ is when the "maximal" q_1 (which minimizes the slack) leads to a loss for Bazza. This is a typical "balance" game.
